# Answers

1 See text, Chapter 1.   
2 See text, Chapter 1.

3 High stocks result from poor control, and poor service results from poor management of supply and forecasts. The better the inventory management, the better the availability and the less stock cover is required.

4   

<table><tr><td>Annual accounts data</td><td>Ace Distributors, $million</td><td>Deuce Associates, $million</td></tr><tr><td>Annual profit</td><td>1.8</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>Return on sales</td><td>1.8</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>8</td><td>26</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>= 22.5%</td><td>19.2%</td></tr><tr><td>Assets are</td><td>2 + 4</td><td>2 + 4</td></tr><tr><td>Return on assets</td><td>1.8</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>6</td><td>13</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>= 30.0%</td><td>38.5%</td></tr></table>

Ace has better margin and more potential for improvement in stock control.

5 $51.4\%$   
6   

<table><tr><td>Annual accounts data</td><td>Ace Distributors, $million</td></tr><tr><td>Annual profit</td><td>1.8</td></tr><tr><td>Return on sales</td><td>1.8</td></tr><tr><td>Assets are</td><td>2 + 1.5</td></tr><tr><td>Return on assets</td><td>1.8</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>3.5</td></tr><tr><td>=</td><td>51.4%</td></tr></table>

Capital employed before reduction $=$ Stock + Capital Plant

$$
= \$ 1. 5 \text {m i l l o n} + \$ 1. 1 \text {m i l l o n}
$$

$$
= \$ 2.6 \text {m i l l o n}
$$

Capital employed after reduction = $2.6 million - $500 000

$$
= \$ 2. 1 \text {m i l l o n}
$$

Return on capital before reduction = $400 000/$2 600 000

$$
= 15 \%
$$

Return on capital after reduction = $400 000/$2 100 000

$$
= 19 \%
$$

7 (a) Key features of the organisation should be:

Integrated inventory system

Central control of inventory

Enclosed and controlled local stores

(see Chapter 13)

(b) Increase in service level will increase the safety stock

CSF for $85\%$ customer service is 1.3 in MADs

CSF for $95\%$ customer service is 2.06 in MADs

The increase in stock required to give

the improved service is therefore $2.06 / 1.3 = 58\%$

The stock required in each of the 12 stores will be enough to cover between the deliveries (i.e. 1 week's usage) plus safety stock. If the total usage rate is 'D', then the usage at each store is D/12. As the MAD only reduces as the square root of the volume then the average stock at each local store will be:

Average local stock $=$ Half weekly usage + Safety stock (Half

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {D} / 1 2) + \mathrm {C S F} \times \mathrm {M A D} / (\text {S q u a r e} \\ \times 1 \mathrm {D} / 2 4 + 0. 3 8 ^ {\star} \mathrm {M A D} \\ \end{array}
$$

Assuming that availability at local stores is still $85\%$

12 local stocks $= 0.5^{\star}\mathrm{D} + 4.5^{\star}\mathrm{MAD}$

Average central stock $= \mathbf{D} + 2.06^{\star}\mathbf{MAD}^{\star}$ (square root of 4)

$$
D + 4. 1 2 ^ {\star} M A D
$$

Total stock $=$ Average central + Average total local

$$
\begin{array}{l} = D + 4. 1 2 ^ {\star} M A D + 0. 5 D + 4. 5 ^ {\star} M A D \\ = 1. 5 \mathrm {D} + 8. 6 2 ^ {\star} \mathrm {M A D} \\ \end{array}
$$

Calculation of new stock value:

Using ABC analysis and the figures in the question:

<table><tr><td></td><td>Av stock/item</td><td>Total stock value</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">A Stock = 236.2</td><td>153 530</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">B Class = 247.4</td><td>123 700</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">C Class = 15.76</td><td>33 096</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">TOTAL per 100 stock lines</td><td>310 326</td></tr></table>

Original stock values was:

$$
\mathrm {D} / 2 + 1. 3 ^ {\star} \mathrm {M A D} ^ {\star} (\text {S q u a r e r o o t 4})
$$

<table><tr><td></td><td>Av stock/item</td><td>Total stock value</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">A Stock = 76</td><td>49 400</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">B Class = 77</td><td>38 500</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">C Class = 4.98</td><td>10 458</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">TOTAL per 100 stock lines</td><td>98 358</td></tr></table>

The change in inventory was therefore from $98358 to$ 310326 an increase of 315.5%.

11 (a)

$\mathrm{TSL} = \mathrm{Demand}$ in lead time and review period plus safety stock in 1T and RP

$$
\begin{array}{l} = 2 0 0 ^ {\star} (4 + 1) + 1. 3 ^ {\star} 5 0 ^ {\star} \left(\text {S q u a r e r o o t} (4 + 1)\right) \\ = 1 0 0 0 + 1 4 5. 3 \\ = 1 1 4 5. 3 \\ \end{array}
$$

(b) Assuming that the lead time is still 4 weeks and orders are placed daily:

$$
\begin{array}{l} = 2 0 0 ^ {\star} (4 + 0. 2) + 1. 3 ^ {\star} 5 0 ^ {\star} \left(\text {S q u a r e} \operatorname {r o o t} (4 + 0. 2)\right) \\ = 8 4 0 + 1 3 3. 2 \\ = 9 7 3. 2 \\ \end{array}
$$

27 The reorder level is used to trigger new replenishment orders when the stock + supply orders outstanding become low. If the delivery frequency is shorter than the lead time then the stock is low, delivery quantities are small and there are always supply orders outstanding. Hence for long lead time A class items ordering is frequent and stock is always less than the review level.   
28 See text, Chapter 7.   
29 a) Stock level depends mainly on supply lead time, customer service and demand pattern. Lead time variation is only as the square

root. (However, delivery performance may be lead time dependent also.)

b) Average $= 13$

$\mathbf{MAD} = 3.33$

Customer service factor is 1.6

Safety stock $= 1.6 \times 3.33 \times \mathrm{SQRT}(2) = 7.53$

Reorder level $= 13 \times 2 + 7.53 = 33.53$

c) Continuous demand

Continuation of the pattern

Normal distribution and non-seasonal

34 (a) Extrinsic forecasts to give causes of demand Market analysis and surveys Historical techniques

Moving average

OK as demand is random and varying much

Exponential smoothing

Better since there could be a slight trend

Double exponential

Will be over-reactive until a trend is seen

Base series

Not appropriate as seasonality not established

Regression

No advantage over other methods

More sophisticated techniques

Insufficient data for these

(For details see text in Chapter 10)

42 a) Independent demand

Assume that a $99.2\%$ customer service level is required.

Average demand $= 12$

MAD $= 4.4$

Customer service factor 3

Safety stock $= 3 \times 4.4 \times$ square root (1)

$$
= 1 3. 4
$$

Delivery quantity $= 12$

Average stock = Half delivery quantity + safety stock

$$
\begin{array}{l} = 6 + 1 3. 4 \\ = 1 9. 4 \\ \end{array}
$$

b) Dependent MRP demand (level 2)

Assuming no safety stock is required, stock would be:

<table><tr><td>Week</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>Demand</td><td>10</td><td>20</td><td>5</td><td>10</td><td>15</td><td></td></tr></table>

Average stock $= 12$

c) JIT supply

Assume that demand is the same every day. (Alternative assumptions are that there should be a safety stock calculated as (a) or as one day lead time.)

<table><tr><td>Week</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>Demand</td><td>10</td><td>20</td><td>5</td><td>10</td><td>15</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Daily demand</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td></td></tr></table>

Assuming that demand is despatched every day:

Average stock $=$ half day's demand:

1 2 0.5 1 1.5

So average stock during the period is 1.2 (assuming a one day delivery interval).
